B0016OLC5Q|3.0|I am familiar enough with this incident to know that the movie makes several factual errors, perhaps intentionally for dramatic purpose.  What I am struck by, however, is the perhaps unintentional layering of Penn's generational angst onto Chris McCandless.  They both came of age in an era where too much attention was given to their emotions by their parents and extended family.  Over time, this generation came to imagine their hurts, pains and emotions were more and greater than others.  I know that is somewhat true of Penn, and he has pasted that over McCandless.  We don't know McCandless well enough to trust that interpretation.  I think Sean Penn admired McCandless's willingness to throw himself into situations he didn't know he could handle, and so he glorified that.  McCandless was looking for adventure, and he found one, although not with the ending he imagined.  I don't think movie directors do the viewing public any justice when they change facts and personalities to make a better movie.  Whether or not Penn meant to portray McCandless as slightly touched, perhaps mentally ill, I was frequently left with that impression.  I also was left with the impression of a spoiled wealthy child who took his own emotions too seriously.  As did all of his family, according to this film.  If we use this film to \\"know\\" McCandless, he also comes across as naive, simplistic, and reckless, as well as someone who arrogantly overestimates his own abilities.  I, for one, would never have shot that moose without having a fire pit waiting and ready with an ample supply of wood.  While the storyline is interesting, as presented, and the photography beautiful, I am uneasy with the creative license Penn took with the life-and death-of a real human being.  Watch with caution would be my final words, and then do a little further research online to see where this film departs from the facts.

0345803485|1.0|Let me preface this by saying that I've read a crap ton of books. Some good, some bad, some 'meh', some amazing. And occasionally I cross a speedbump of a travesty like 50 Shades.Only twice in the past have I actively tossed a book and given up on reading any further. The first was Stephen Donaldson's \\"White Gold Wielder\\", in which the protagonist arrives from Earth onto a fantasy world, discovers his leprosy has been healed... and promptly celebrates/tests his healed flesh by sexually assaulting a girl in a stable. The second was one of Laurell K. Hamilton's \\"Anita Blake, Vampire Hump--er, I mean, Hunter\\", because the plot had long since stopped being a plot for multiple books past and replaced by supernaturally ginned-up IKEA porn. BAD IKEA porn.    So, getting through this book I consider to be an endurance test. I wanted to set it alight many times and dance around its flames chanting the names of the Great Old Ones. I wanted to fire it from a cannon aimed across the Atlantic and into EL James' brainpan. I wanted to dial up the publisher and ask \\"What In Hell Is Wrong With You People?!\\"    Reading this book was by turns annoying, painful, disgusting, and pathetic.    If you're looking for quality literature, you won't find it here. If you're looking for an enrapturing love story, you won't find it here. If you're looking for solid three-dimensional characters, engaging writing, or anything resembling sanity, a believeable narrative, or even a semi-believable one, you sure as hell won't find those here either.    What you WILL find, however, is the 'story' of a mousy, barely-there shell of a girl with a preposterous lack of any sexual or personal agency (seriously, as the book opens, she's never dated, never gotten drunk, never had a boyfriend, never had sex, made out, or even masturbated, doesn't have an email account [?!]... and she's a college graduate). In short order, she is snapped up by a \\"Dom\\" who fulfills all that an impressionable reader's expectationsof what a charming \\"real man\\" should be. She's easily terrified of him (and given his scary attitude, no wonder), by turns pathetic and pedantic in her 'journey' through this book, and her subconscious and 'inner goddess' (apparently two different things) act in a retch-inducing manner to match the character they're part of. I can only assume that the 'love' she develops for him, after his creepy behavior, his constant stalking, and jerk-tastic behavior is similar to how hostages and prisoners develop Stockholm Syndrome and feel 'love' for their captors.    And so what is so grand about this epitome of arsehattery? Christian Grey is rich, handsome, well-made... and a completely unloveable pathetic turd, an execrable excuse for a human being. His temper flares at the slightest provocation, his behavior--composed of random over-the-top gift-giving, displays of extravagance, emotional ups and downs, and creepy pursuit behavior--is very much reminiscent of psychological and emotional traits found by actors in abusive relationships. The fact that he's apparently kinky because of his mental issues makes a mockery of anyone who's in the lifestyles of leather, kink, or BDSM, thus furthering the stereotype of what TVTropes might call the Depraved Dominant if they had such an entry on that trope.    As it is, I did a little research on mental disorders, and according to the DSM (the standard Diagnostic and Statistical Manual, the book by which psychologists study and define various mental illnesses), Christian Grey fits virtually ALL of the criteria for someone with Narcissistic Personality Disorder, ranging from invariably angry reactions to criticism, and constant needs for attention, to becoming (VERY) easily jealous and having (Obviously) trouble keeping healthy relationships.    As an aside, his constant referral to the female dominant who initiated him into BDSM as a teen: This would not only count as statutory rape and molestation on a continued, intentional basis (aswell as her own unfaithfulness to her husband who was unaware and unconsenting of such a relationship), but yet again paints BDSM types as depraved freaks. To me, I find this deeply insulting and badly stereotyped, and his protestations to the reader and to Ana about how \\"It wasn't like that!\\" ring hollow in and out of the story.    As an aside to the aside, the reversal of this sort of thing, wherein a teenaged female were taken into the 'care' of a much older man (especially the sexual and sadomasochistic portions), would be reviled as incredibly disgusting and horrific (as it should be).    Or, it would be Twilight.    (Which is what this was, originally: Twilight fan-fiction with the serial numbers filed off.)    Overall, EL James has essentially written a book about an abusive relationship, under the guise of kink. I can't think of a single sane individual with a kinky side to them who would find this sort of 'relationship' acceptable. This book promotes multiple bad stereotypes. The first, of course, of people being kinky as a consequence or parallel to being screwed up in the head: Grey was abused as a child, and Mrs. Robinson, his domme, was okay with breaking the law and having sex with a minor. The second being that abusive relationships are okay, because they're not REALLY abusive, they're just different (I don't call living in constant fear of your partner a healthy D/s relationship at all, no matter their 'charms). And possibly the third being that one can redeem someone simply by loving them.    Sorry kids, none of this tripe works that way in the real world unless you find yourself part of a very unhealthy relationship.    I realize that there are readers out there who think this book is the greatest thing since Lady Chatterley's Lover. I realize that there are no shortage of people out there who have various kinks, and may not realize it until they have an enlightening experience or exposure to something that triggers those feelings. I realize that these two traits may intersect. But really, I find it deplorable beyond belief that the two had to intersect here, with this travesty. There are so many other good pieces of writing, and film, and other media that promote and explore kink in the context of healthy relationships, or even ones that change over time in a more believable manner.    I won't be reading the next two in the series. I hear they get better. I also hear that they shift from first to third person (which in a book series counts as a poor thing to do in my opinion, unless your intent was to shift between a large cast of characters constantly). I hear they have a happy ending. I also hear they have helicopter crashes, psycho submissives with knives, and other 'plot' points you'd see in the most pedantic of daytime soap operas.    Essentially, this book is just that: A terrible, ridiculous, pathetic, time-and-money-and-sanity-wasting, badly-portrayed-BDSM-laced soap opera. I will not give it a letter grade, and I only assign it 1 star because Amazon won't let me rate it in the negatives. This book insults the grading system itself. I will only say that I am glad never to read it again, because if they make this into a film I'll end up feeling like Sam Neill at the conclusion of \\"In The Mouth Of Madness\\" as the world collapses into insanity and chaos. I pray to Odin, Osiris, Amaterasu, Ogoun, and any other deity out there that they don't actually go that far.    But I know they will. In England, this prat EL James and her publishers are laughing all the way to the bank, and I hate every single one of them for it.

630289915X|5.0|Here I am, just another reviewer acknowledging the immortal significance of this gritty but sentimental, sometimes harsh but more often joyous, deliberate but life-affirming treasure that is \\"It's a Wonderful Life\\" (hereafter \\"Wonderful Life\\").  Do I have a new perspective on \\"Wonderful Life\\"?  Maybe, but I doubt it, but here goes:  It has often struck me how \\"Wonderful Life\\" is sort of the inversion and distorted reflection of \\"A Christmas Carol\\".<br /><br />  George Bailey and Ebenezer Scrooge - can any two characters be more diametrically opposed? George is kind-hearted, thoughtful of others, beloved by the citizenry; Ebenezer is uncaring, misanthropic, and treated with fearful disregard.  The only thing they seem to have in common is that both are industrious and, surprisingly, both dissatisfied with their lots.  However, although rich with public esteem, George admits his despondent dissatisfaction with his relatively \\"poor\\" financial status. Conversely, although rich with material goods, Ebenezer harbors dissatisfaction with his relatively poor social/human status, that is, his alienation, though he will hardly admit this.  In a subtle, distorted way, George and Ebenezer kinda want what the other has - the only difference is, ironically, what Ebenezer subconsciously wants is better than what George wants.<br /><br />  Of course, both men receive spiritual intervention and of course, get a glimpse of their futures.  Only difference is, George receives a vision of what the future would be if he did not continue his altruistic ways, while Scrooge receives a vision of what the future would be if he DID continue his selfish, parsimonious ways.  For both men, both visions promise stark oblivion (in different ways, of course).  Both men's future visions, for different reasons, convince them that selfless interaction with society is the path to bliss; George just needed to be reminded of it, while Ebenezer needed a remedial course.<br /><br />  Therefore, in my opinion, many parallelisms and common themes exist between \\"Wonderful Life\\" and \\"Christmas Carol\\" besides the obvious fact that both tales are Christmas (and universal) classics.n, many parallelisms and common themes exist between \\"Wonderful Life\\" and \\"Christmas Carol\\" besides the obvious fact that both tales are Christmas (and universal) classics.

B0086449YA|4.0|Mark Knopfler's seventh solo album is his first double-CD release, but the quality and diversity of its 20 original songs amply justify issuing two discs. He toured Europe with Bob Dylan last year and will tour with him in North America this fall, so it's tempting to speculate that working alongside the world's most eminent living songwriter may have sharpened Mark's game and made him more prolific. (They've been playing and recording together off and on since 1979.)<br /><br />In interviews, Knopfler likens traveling with a band to sailing the world with a crew of brigands, which is what privateers once did during times of war. They were authorized by governments to attack foreign cargo ships, plundering them for booty in order to weaken the enemy. They didn't sink vessels, only commandeered them, and this is akin to what Mark and his band do when they play, capturing new fans who who join those that have followed him since the days of Dire Straits.<br /><br />The songs on \\"Privateering\\" are consistently low-key, character-driven, beautifully written stories of scoundrels on the run and people on the down-and-out, including the opening track, \\"Redbud Tree\\": \\"I crouch in dread / Discovery my certain death / Her leaves reaching for my head / As I suspend my breath / Redbud tree shelter me, shelter me.\\"<br /><br />The song \\"Haul Away\\" introduces a nautical sound, complete with a Celtic penny whistle, fiddle, and accordion, and a theme of lost love. The album then shifts to a great blues tune, \\"Don't Forget Your Hat,\\" featuring steel slide guitar and harmonica: \\"Look out the window / Never saw so much rain / You better get down to the station / If you wanna catch that train / So long, I guess that's that / Hey hey, don't forget your hat.\\"<br /><br />From trains, Knopfler returns to a song of the sea on the title track, which makes it clear that privateers were little better than pirates. The charmingly simple \\"Miss You Blues\\" follows (though it's not actually a blues), as does the gently rocking \\"Corn Beef City,\\" a truck-driving song that reflects the hard economic times we're living through: \\"You don't ask questions when there's nothing in the bank / You gotta feed the kids and put the diesel in the tank.\\"<br /><br />As one might expect, \\"Go, Love\\" is a sad song of farewell, while \\"Hot or What\\" switches back to the blues for some amusing first-person braggin' and harmonica blowin' from a lucky gambler. The first CD closes with \\"Yon Two Crows,\\" the tale of a muddy shepherd, and the touching, evocative \\"Seattle,\\" about a pair of disillusioned dreamers in my rainy hometown: \\"I still believe that there's somewhere for us / But now it's something that we don't discuss / You're the best thing I ever knew / Stay with me, baby, and we'll make it to / Seattle.\\"<br /><br />Opening disc two is a second Celtic melody entitled \\"Kingdom of Gold.\\" Its lyrics describe the dire fate of buccaneers who attack a powerful medieval king: \\"His axes and armor will conquer these devils / The turbulent raiders will falter and fall / Their leaders be taken, their camps burned and leveled / They'll hang in the wind from his citadel walls.\\"<br /><br />Next up is \\"Got to Have Something,\\" a basic blues tune about whiskey and guns, succeeded by the wistful, jazz-tinged \\"Radio City Serenade,\\" a nostalgic love song to New York City, and \\"I Used to Could,\\" a lively honky-tonk number about a bad boy and his GMC Cannonball truck. \\"Gator Blood\\" is Southern-fried roots music with wailing pedal steel guitar, while \\"Bluebird\\" is another melancholy blues about a farmer who's run out of luck. The beautiful gloom continues with \\"Dream of the Drowned Submariner\\" and \\"Blood and Water,\\" but Knopfler finally allows us a bit of happiness on the blues shuffle \\"Today Is OK,\\" in which a boxer addresses his girlfriend:<br /><br />\\"Baby, you can read my mind / I'll be home just after nine<br />You're the best medicine I know / Tonight we're gonna let it all go<br />Put on that pretty silk shirt / Put on that tight black skirt<br />Put on those heels so black / We can party on when I get back\\"<br /><br />The album ends with a cute bit of Americana that looks at Jack \\"After the Bean Stalk.\\" Like much of \\"Privateering,\\" it's country and bluesy, warm and wry. Nothing flashy, just good, comfortable music you can relax into.ty silk shirt / Put on that tight black skirt<br />Put on those heels so black / We can party on when I get back\\"<br /><br />The album ends with a cute bit of Americana that looks at Jack \\"After the Bean Stalk.\\" Like much of \\"Privateering,\\" it's country and bluesy, warm and wry. What is the Holy Grail?) and turning into a fast-paced thriller that questions the basis of several widely held assumptions. B003VANOFY|4.0|I work from home. I write for a living. I spend ALL day every day at the computer writing -- and my favorite way to relax at home is playing games. While I cringe to admit it, it's fair to say that I might spend more time with these headphones on my head on a given day than I spend with them off. If anybody has stress-tested these things...it's me.<br /><br />This is my second pair of G930s, and before I bought the G930s, I had the G35s, which are essentially just the wired version.<br /><br />They aren't without problems. My first pair went into the trash when the plastic fitting at the top of the ear cup on the left side -- into which the metal size adjustment rail is slotted -- simply broke. The plastic had gotten tired with time (and my taking the headphones on and off), and shattered in such a way that it would have been impossible to fix it without a lot of tape. Tape + proximity to my hair = no thanks. Aside from that structural failure, the headphones were fine; they still sounded great, and the mic worked perfectly. (Prior to buying logitech headphones, I had a pair of Razer headphones, and they also broke at the top of the ear cup, so I don't think that this is a problem exclusive to the G930s -- I think anything you take on and off of your head repeatedly, with swiveling plastic parts that can be torqued, is at some risk of breaking eventually.)<br /><br />This second pair of headphones has lost tension on the microphone arm -- whatever mechanism inside allowed it to have tension to raise and lower in different positions is no longer situated properly, so the mic sags. I've rigged it by tucking a bit of folded paper behind the 'elbow' of the mic where it joins the cup, but needless to say, it isn't ideal. Aside from that, the headphones work properly.<br /><br />Both headphones have had some weird issues with turning on and off when plugged in. This is an issue mainly when used with windows, as the headphones will automatically turn off after a certain length of time if they receive no incoming sounds (or possibly any input on the mic). When that happens, I find it easiest to unplug the headset, turn it back on, and then plug it back in.<br /><br />Obviously, I liked these enough to buy a second pair despite the price tag and minor issues. For my purposes, they're a great fit: they're comfortable to wear for long periods of time; they sit comfortably around my ears rather than pressing on them. They're a little bit heavy, but most headphones of this size will have some weight -- and whatever battery these have in them usually gives me eight or more continuous hours of use before I have to plug them in (which isn't a huge inconvenience, because the charging station and cord are fairly lengthy). The 7.1 is really well done, with a what feels to me like an expansive soundstage that's *great* for gaming -- I never have trouble pinpointing where in the 3D space a certain sound is coming from. The mic is good...almost too sensitive, as sometimes the people I'm in vent with will tell me that the sound of my sink running sounds like a garbage disposal! Generally, though, they say the quality of output is clear and solid.<br /><br />The programmable keys on the side are pretty useful, though I wouldn't mind if the driver software were updated to include other programs like Skype or Mumble.<br /><br />A few last points: sometimes, USB headphones, particularly surround sound USB headphones, do not get along with certain game titles. There can be weird static, dropped sounds, or other shenanigans, and though this is really rare, it's pretty frustrating if, like me, you rely on the headphones entirely for sound (living as I do in an apartment with neighbors who probably wouldn't appreciate listening to virtual gunfire late in the evening). Occasionally, the driver software will decrease the mic volume on its own, or scroll the headset volume downward, without being told to. Little problems like this are usually easily fixed by unplugging the USB receiver and plugging it back in to refresh the device (and then turning the headset on fresh afterward).<br /><br />None of these are dealbreakers. I'll admit I've done a little bit of research on other wireless gaming headsets in light of these little nagging issues, but I have yet to find one that offered me the same utility and sound quality as this pair of headphones does. If you're willing to deal with a wired connection, I think there are probably better deals out there...but if you're looking for a set of excellent wireless headphones, I think as of the writing of this review, you can't really do any better than the G930s.nplugging the USB receiver and plugging it back in to refresh the device (and then turning the headset on fresh afterward).<br /><br />None of these are dealbreakers. B0014WSWTY|3.0|Another original-instrument performance of Beethoven's symphonies.  What makes these different from the many other such cycles currently available on disc centers around three points, as set forth in a fascinating explanatory essay by Immerseel found in the booklet: 1)  These not only use \\"original instruments,\\" but instruments specific to the Vienna of Beethoven's time, allegedly different-sounding from and pitched higher than those used in other cities in Europe.  2)  Extensive research into musical performances in the Vienna of that time, leading to an orchestra size ranging from the high thirties to the low forties (as opposed to the sixty or so players used in other original-instrument cycles).  3) Eschewing the idea of creative \\"interpretation\\" of the music, in favor of the \\"declamation\\" practices of the time, based on a strictly literal following of the composer's tempi and dynamics.<br /><br />So, does it succeed?  On the first two points, quite well indeed.  Under Immerseel's direction, Anima Eterna produce a \\"big\\" sound that belies the small number of players -- in terms of sonic impact, what we hear on this set is every bit the equal of the \\"big orchestra\\" sound of Beethoven cycles from leading orchestras such as the Berlin Philharmonic or Chicago Symphony.  The sound of the Viennese \\"instrumentarium\\" is also mostly a smashing success, with richly colored woodwinds and quicksilver strings.  Only the brass disappoints a bit; granted, all Classical-era brass instruments pale somewhat when compared to their modern equivalents, but the horns in Anima Eterna, in particular, don't quite hit a smooth pitch at far too many moments in this cycle, the solo passage in the third movement of the Ninth being a prime example.  Overall, though, there is much to admire in the sound of the instrumental blend.<br /><br />As to the performances, unfortunately, they're somewhat more hit-or-miss.  On the good side, Immerseel and his forces turn in an excellent Eroica, full offire and passion.  Likewise, their readings of the Seventh and Eighth symphonies are first-rate, as are the overtures included in the set, with the Second and Fourth not too far behind.  The Ninth receives a good-if-not-great reading, although a bit brusque and superficial in the slow movement.  In the finale, the soloists and \\"Turkish band\\" are world-class, but the twenty-four-voice chorus, although fine when singing together in full voice, sounds rather anemic when only two sections at a time are singing, such as the men in the first statement of the \\"Ode to Joy.\\"  The First, on the other hand, seemed to have a rather characterless, if inoffensive, performance.<br /><br />It is in the remaining two major mid-period works, the Fifth and Pastoral, where things go seriously off-the-rails.  The famed opening movement of the Fifth is simply taken far too fast; instead of sounding thrilling (as with Norrington's just-teetering-on-the-edge-of-control version), this rendition just makes one of the most important creations in classical music sound rushed and superficial.  Things improve in the final three movements, but here the playing is not at its best, with far too many mushy attacks, particularly in the final two movements, marring much of the drama.  The Pastoral also suffers from a too-fast opening movement (which, as far as I can tell, is even faster than Beethoven's specified 66-per-half-note tempo), moments of surprising torpor (in particular, a lack of incisiveness in crucial instrumental accents) in the admittedly-loud storm, and a general lack of the \\"expression of feeling\\" in the presence of nature which was Beethoven's whole point in this work.  If, as one commentator observed, Karajan's view of nature in his much-maligned 1963 Pastoral was less that of strolling through the forest so much as speeding along a country road in his Porsche, Immerseel's view seems to be that of zooming over the treetops at Mach 2 in a fighter jet.  One may well wonder how much these shortcomings related to Immerseel's dedication to \\"declamation\\" over \\"interpretation\\" -- that he does a good job of getting the tempo and dynamics right, and thus gets fine results with works where \\"everything is on the page\\" and can be gleaned from the composer's explicit directions, but, when it comes to more-subjective matters involved in getting to the heart of pieces when said essence is found \\"between the lines\\" in the score, rather than clearly marked out in terms of tempo and dynamics, he can get a bit lost at sea.<br /><br />The orchestral sound captured by producer/engineer Bert van der Wolf is rich and glowing, if growing somewhat congested at loud climaxes.  (If you're an audiophile looking purely for the original-instrument cycle with the best sound quality, this is your easy choice.)  According to the booklet, this was an \\"Original High Resolution Surround Recording\\" (I'm assuming on SACD), and may have sounded even better in surround-sound; however, I'm not aware of any place where this cycle is available in anything above Redbook stereo.<br /><br />So, in summary, a mixed bag.  If the symphonies in this set were available separately, several of them (particularly the Eroica, Seventh, and Eighth) would be an easy recommendation.  As a complete set, though, it falls short in too many key works to be anything other than an \\"interesting\\" supplement to other, more-satisfying cycles.  My personal recommendations would be for the original-instrument Gardiner/OR&R set, Barenboim's \\"big orchestra\\" version with the Staatskapelle Berlin or, if you can only pick one set, Haitink's cycle with the London Symphony, which balances the use of a modern orchestra with \\"historically-informed\\" performance practice in a satisfying combination, and which culminates in one of the finest Ninths of all time.

Connnelly is a master and his descriptions of L.A. are flawless and right on track. Having grown up in Los Angeles the places brought back many memories with his decriptions of places. This book is one you can read relatively quickly and that is why I chose it. I wanted to be sure that I liked the author and Harry Bosch character without investing alot of time. Suspenful, intriquing and fast-paced this book is perfect for someone like me who is new to the Harry Bosch series and character. A detective that is a man's man it is quite easy for me to relate to Bosch in his quest to solve a crime and save Los Angeles in the process. Good stuff and highly recommmended especially for newbies to Harry Bosch.

Later, Joey is forced to perform the grueling and crippling work of a war horse. In some ways, this book could be compared to Black Beauty, also told through the eyes of a horse. But this story focuses on terrible war, and the predicaments that no human or animal should ever have to endure. 0812993292|5.0|I really loved this book.  The writing is exceptional, descriptive and fluid, with so many threads woven into the story you are really drawn in.  The very idea that a meek retired Englishman unaccustomed to doing anything of consequence and who's never walked further than the post box taking off spontaneously on a journey across England seems bizarre. We question his motivations just as his wife does.  His need to reach his dying friend to say goodbye makes perfect sense, but to walk?  And walk so ill-equipped? The story is about his journey from both the physical aspect of what he sees, who he meets and how he holds up, and about his inner journey which unfolds as he walks.  There are parts of himself just as distant as the city to which he is headed. The miles of slow steps allow plenty of time for thinking.  He can talk freely about his trek to strangers, but seems unable to face the depths inside himself.<br /><br />The loveliness of the English countryside comes alive, the goodness of strangers and the resilience of the human body, but what is most touching is how deeply we hide things from ourselves in an effort to live freely, never knowing the cocoon we wrap around ourselves limits our every breath.  Harold starts out appearing as peaceful as one can be, but has to travel all the way across England to learn what real peace is, and all the time he's traveling, his wife, left waiting at home, struggles through her own issues.<br /><br />The story is laid out beautifully, although being near Harold's age (older) and equally limited in conditioning, the distances he travels in a day seems awfully short as even I could do better (although some days I couldn't move).  Some of those types of details (the physical consequences of that effort) are hard to pin down realistically, but don't detract from the reader's need to believe Harold is actually doing it.<br /><br />I often start a book slowly, but if the book is compelling enough, at some point I find myself drawn so deeply into the story I read on till I'm done--sometimes for hours.  This is one of those books.  It's about as far from being a page-turning thriller as you can get, and yet I found myself turning those pages with the same need Harold felt to reach his friend.  Highly recommended.n so deeply into the story I read on till I'm done--sometimes for hours.  This is one of those books. 0786856297|4.0|First off, everyone who says _Lightning Thief_ takes inspiration from Harry Potter is spot on.  Percy Jackson isn't too much like Harry (black hair and green eyes aside), but their circumstances are quite, quite similar even before Percy learns he's a special, magical person and gets whisked off to a special, magical place of learning.  By the time he starts off on his quest for a stolen artifact--after forming a trio with a clever girl and a boy who's a bit of a good-hearted bumbler--the resemblance is uncanny.  If you've read _Harry Potter and the Sorcerer's Stone_ and liked it, you may enjoy this too, as it puts some of the same magic in different clothing.  On the other hand, if you didn't like _Sorcerer's Stone_ I doubt you'd care for Percy's exploits either--unless you're a fan of Greek myth, in which case they're worth a look.<br /><br />Even though it could stand to be more original, the story is pretty fun.  Percy Jackson starts out as a boy whose luck seems uniformly bad, who gets the wrong sort of attention wherever he goes but never quite knows why.  Finding out he's the son of an ancient god tells him 'why' but doesn't do anything to end his problems!  His quest to set out on the path of a hero--never mind rescue Zeus's thunderbolt--involves a lot of action and danger, moments when his courage and caring shine, and times when Percy and his two friends have to face their weaknesses.  The pacing isn't perfect; the quest section of the book has an episodic feel and eventually starts to drag.  But things are still fast-moving and engaging overall.<br /><br />Best yet, Percy's world makes for a pretty good introduction to the Greek gods, goddesses, and heroic figures.  Enough interesting stuff from the myths either gets hinted at or appears outright to intrigue a newcomer to the subject without bogging the story down in details it just doesn't need.<br /><br />I wouldn't say, though, that Percy Jackson has Harry Potter's kind of age-crossover potential.  I didn't find the same charm and wit here that I do in Rowling's work.  Actually, in style it reminded me more of a pre-teen movie than Potter.  There's almost too much action and the lines are often more snappy than clever.  Although I liked it, I would have liked it a lot more when I was Percy's age, so I'm not sure I'd recommend it to adults at all unless they regularly enjoy stuff from the young adult shelves.
